    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer18149057438075203621"><b>In a comic masterpiece following the misadventures of a simple but hugely ambitious waiter in pre-World War II Prague, who rises to wealth only to lose everything with the onset of Communism, Bohumil Hrabal takes us on a tremendously funny and satirical trip through 20th-century Czechoslovakia.</b><br/><br/>First published in 1971 in a typewritten edition, then finally printed in book f</span>
  <span id="freeText18149057438075203621" style="display:none"><b>In a comic masterpiece following the misadventures of a simple but hugely ambitious waiter in pre-World War II Prague, who rises to wealth only to lose everything with the onset of Communism, Bohumil Hrabal takes us on a tremendously funny and satirical trip through 20th-century Czechoslovakia.</b><br /><br />First published in 1971 in a typewritten edition, then finally printed in book form in 1989, I Served the King of England is "an extraordinary and subtly tragicomic novel" (The New York Times), telling the tale of Ditie, a hugely ambitious but simple waiter in a deluxe Prague hotel in the years before World War II. Ditie is called upon to serve not the King of England, but Haile Selassie. It is one of the great moments in his life. Eventually, he falls in love with a Nazi woman athlete as the Germans are invading Czechoslovakia. After the war, through the sale of valuable stamps confiscated from the Jews, he reaches the heights of his ambition, building a hotel. He becomes a millionaire, but with the institution of communism, he loses everything and is sent to inspect mountain roads. Living in dreary circumstances, Ditie comes to terms with the inevitability of his death, and with his place in history.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18149057438075203621" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_1133440951" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1133440951" />
    <a title="Tony" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33771-tony"><img alt="Tony" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1292869016p2/33771.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1133440951?book_show_action=true">Dec 16, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tony" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tony" href="/user/show/33771-tony">Tony</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33771-tony?shelf=top-10-2014">top-10-2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33771-tony?shelf=czech">czech</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1133440951" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14238771475747849455">Czechs say Bohumil Hrabal's work is untranslatable. When I read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/87280.Too_Loud_a_Solitude' title='Too Loud a Solitude by Bohumil Hrabal' rel='nofollow noopener'>Too Loud a Solitude</a> I indeed felt something wasn't coming through. But I chalked it up to a style of Eastern European literature: dark, allegorical, an unfiltered cigarette of protest to communist grime. Glad I read it but no rush to read any more Hrabal.<br/><br/>And then I stopped in a used bookstore, just this week. Monday. There was <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/397503.I_Served_the_King_of_England' title='I Served the King of England by Bohumil Hrabal' rel='nofollow noopener'>I Served the King of England</a>. I jimmied it out and thumbed it open, expecting to read a sentence or two and</span>
  <span id="freeText14238771475747849455" style="display:none">Czechs say Bohumil Hrabal's work is untranslatable. When I read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/87280.Too_Loud_a_Solitude" title="Too Loud a Solitude by Bohumil Hrabal" rel="nofollow noopener">Too Loud a Solitude</a> I indeed felt something wasn't coming through. But I chalked it up to a style of Eastern European literature: dark, allegorical, an unfiltered cigarette of protest to communist grime. Glad I read it but no rush to read any more Hrabal.<br /><br />And then I stopped in a used bookstore, just this week. Monday. There was <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/397503.I_Served_the_King_of_England" title="I Served the King of England by Bohumil Hrabal" rel="nofollow noopener">I Served the King of England</a>. I jimmied it out and thumbed it open, expecting to read a sentence or two and slide it back. Instead I read this:<br /><br /><i>When I started to work at the Golden Prague Hotel, the boss took hold of my left ear, pulled me up, and said, You're a busboy here, so remember, you don't see anything and you don't hear anything. Repeat what I just said. So I said I wouldn't see anything and I wouldn't hear anything. Then the boss pulled my right ear and said, But remember too that you've got to see everything and hear everything. That's how I began.</i><br /><br />I'm only human. So I took it home and rushed to finish what else I was reading. I started Tuesday. I followed Ditie, the busboy, from hotel to hotel. From busboy to waiter. <i>I was always lucky in my bad luck</i>, he said.<br /><br />I'm trying to tell you about this in a way that will not be full of spoilers. <br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Eventually the Germans come, first in the form of a lovely Nazi gym instructor. She weds Ditie, but not before the high comedy of Ditie being examined by Nazi doctors to see if his sperm is acceptable. Their superior blood child may be autistic. What he does, and only does, is pound nails into the floor. Ditie will hear them always. Ditie survives the war, becomes a millionaire hotel owner. By this point he has been a Nazi sympathizer and a beneficiary of commandeered Jewish property. But somehow remains completely lovable. The communists come and take it all away. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br /><br />Well, I guess that didn't work.<br /><br />Instead, let me tell you one anecdote. See, Ditie, as you can imagine, meets many different characters. One is Mr. Šíba, the soccer referee. Nobody wanted to referee the Sparta-Slavia match because the crowd always insulted the referee. So Mr. Šíba agreed to do it. He practiced by running through the birch trees, <i>reprimanding and threatening Burger and Braine with expulsion, but mostly yelling at Mr. Říha, One more time and you're out of the game</i>. A headwaiter took a bus of inmates from an asylum, along with a barrel of beer, to watch. That small story can not have anything but a happy ending.<br /><br />_____ _____ _____ _____ _____<br /><br />Eventually, Ditie realizes this: <i>although the stars were visible at night, at noon you could see them only from the bottom of a deep well.</i> So, like Murakami, he goes for introspection. The book did not wane here.<br /><br />_____ _____ _____ _____ _____<br /><br />See, I've been doing this thing with reading friends, real life reading friends. We meet sometime before Christmas, in some place that serves alcoholic beverages, and, in turn, announce our top ten books of the year list. This started with two guys on bar napkins and no preparation, to seven, down to a more manageable five, but with hand-out typed lists. Civility moderates. Tonight, Thursday, was that night. I was pretty satisfied with my list about a month ago. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77287.Independent_People" title="Independent People by Halldór Laxness" rel="nofollow noopener">Independent People</a> had been on the bubble but then I read Mo Yan's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13563087.Frog" title="Frog by Mary Calmes" rel="nofollow noopener">Frog</a> and I thought I was set. But as the weeks passed, I weighed which books resonated most, and Iceland appeared to be the final journey. <br /><br />But, as I said, I started <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/397503.I_Served_the_King_of_England" title="I Served the King of England by Bohumil Hrabal" rel="nofollow noopener">I Served the King of England</a> Tuesday. By the time Ditie had to masturbate for the Nazi doctors (Wednesday on my calendar), I had to see whether he passed the ethnic purity test. I wasn't howling. Just smiling on every page. Who says Hrabal can't be translated?<br /><br />So I woke this morning, 120 pages to go. It could be the <i>first</i> book next year. But readers, you know how it is.<br /><br />Iceland Sagas and Chinese one-child policy novels would have to be runners-up. This was too good not to make the list. How often do I give 5 stars?</span>
  <a data-text-id="14238771475747849455" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_933283363" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/933283363" />
    <a title="Nicole~" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20071931-nicole"><img alt="Nicole~" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1439784920p2/20071931.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/933283363?book_show_action=true">May 10, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nicole~" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nicole~" href="/user/show/20071931-nicole">Nicole~</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20071931-nicole?shelf=czech-republic">czech-republic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20071931-nicole?shelf=hrabal">hrabal</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20071931-nicole?shelf=lit-fiction">lit-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer933283363" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13354161938276441265"><b>Man's body and spirit are indestructible...he is merely changed or metamorphized</b><br/>- Bohumil Hrabal, <i>I Served the King of England</i> (1971).<br/><br/>Hrabal's satirically political, erotically imagined and poignant adventure story follows the rise and fall of a young busboy Ditie (Czech for 'child') who, despite his diminutive stature, possesses big dreams and the determination to become a millionaire to be <i>the equal of everyone else</i>: is influenced by his father's advice to have <i>an aim in life</i> because then he'd</span>
  <span id="freeText13354161938276441265" style="display:none"><b>Man's body and spirit are indestructible...he is merely changed or metamorphized</b><br />- Bohumil Hrabal, <i>I Served the King of England</i> (1971).<br /><br />Hrabal's satirically political, erotically imagined and poignant adventure story follows the rise and fall of a young busboy Ditie (Czech for 'child') who, despite his diminutive stature, possesses big dreams and the determination to become a millionaire to be <i>the equal of everyone else</i>: is influenced by his father's advice to have <i>an aim in life</i> because then he'd have a reason for living, initiates his rise at the brothel 'Paradise'- a habit-forming place that, from Ditie's innocent viewpoint, is <i>so wonderful and forbidden that I wanted nothing more in this world,... because at last I'd found a beautiful and noble aim.</i><br /><br />His adventures surrealistically course through farcical scenes of life in wartime Prague, though he stumbles upward without much thought to moral implications, idealizing and fervently learning from the distinguished headwaiter who knows everything about everything because, as the latter puts it: "<i>I served the King of England.</i>" Ditie's steps to elevated stature are vividly imagined, particularly in one ironic episode where he effortlessly fumbles into serving the Emperor of Ethiopia, Hailie Selassie: the similarity in their physical sizes is not lost on the reader.<br /><blockquote>
  <i>I learned that feeling victorious makes you victorious, and that once you lose heart or let yourself be discouraged the feeling of defeat will stay with you for the rest of your life, and you'll never get back on your feet again, especially in your own country and your own surroundings, where you're considered a runt, an eternal busboy. </i>
</blockquote><br />Hrabal depicts scenes of the German Occupation of Czechoslovakia through Ditie's relationship with Lise, a Nazi gym-instructor. Their marriage is expected to help perpetuate the future Aryan pure-blooded offspring of the Reich. Hrabal's highly erotic treatment depicting Germany in 'the throes of swallowing up' Czechoslovakia, of the latter forced to submission and, under pressure too, the ejaculation of its own identity proves this author's mastery of allegory. The handicapped nature of Ditie's and Lise's child is another ironic, powerfully piercing twist of the dagger in Hitler's ideology.<br /><br />With Hrabal's signature taste for the absurd direction of <i>Fate</i>, his hero becomes the millionaire of his dreams but through the establishment of communism, loses that long sought-for stature, along with the freedom he thought it would afford him.<br /><br />The final portions of Ditie's story are the most poignant, with a theme that the author often uses in his novels: memories, self reflection, and the expectations of one's life. Ditie becomes a road mender, and in his loneliness, he contemplates the direction he let his life take, coming to some profound conclusions of integrity and morality. <br /><blockquote>
  <i>..the maintenance of this road was the maintenance of my own life, which now, when I looked back on it, seemed to have happened to someone else. My life at this point seemed like a novel, a book written by a stranger even though I alone had the key to it, I alone was a witness to it, even though my life too was constantly being overgrown by grass and weeds at either end. But as I used a grub hoe and a shovel on the road, I used memory to keep the road of my life open into the past, so I could take my thoughts backward to where I wanted to begin remembering...and so arriving, in this back and forth way, at the meaning of life. Not the meaning of what used to be or what happened a long time ago, but discovering the kind of road you'd opened up and had yet to open up, and whether there was still time to attain the serenity that would secure you against the desire to escape from your own solitude, from the most important questions that you ask yourself.</i>
</blockquote><br />Hrabal's tragicomic tale of a nobody seeking to become important, and Czechoslovakia's pre-WWII to its Communism period may be one and the same. Ditie's introspective thoughts of his journey; of interrogating, accusing, and defending himself; and of finally moving forward, may be taken as reflective of Hrabal's beloved country. This highly inventive, equally erotic, funny, tragic, philosophical and inspiring work deeply touched and entertained. Just a word of caution, though..the movie was disappointing.<br /><br />Read May 15th, 2014<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13354161938276441265" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_526816469" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/526816469" />
    <a title="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan"><img alt="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1553101944p2/4728718.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/526816469?book_show_action=true">Feb 04, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" href="/user/show/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan">Algernon (Darth Anyan)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan?shelf=2013">2013</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer526816469" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11457099063340944885"> <br/>I was actually trying to find another book by Hrabal : <b> Closely Watched Trains </b>, but the library only had this. I'm glad I picked it up, as it was a joy to read from start to finish, and much more serious and thought provoking than the comedy of the first pages let me believe. Probably, the book would not qualify as a 'hidden gem' with a 4+ rating from 4000 votes and an inclusion into one of those '1001 books to read before you die' lists, but I still think it is underappreciated and worthy of </span>
  <span id="freeText11457099063340944885" style="display:none"> <br />I was actually trying to find another book by Hrabal : <b> Closely Watched Trains </b>, but the library only had this. I'm glad I picked it up, as it was a joy to read from start to finish, and much more serious and thought provoking than the comedy of the first pages let me believe. Probably, the book would not qualify as a 'hidden gem' with a 4+ rating from 4000 votes and an inclusion into one of those '1001 books to read before you die' lists, but I still think it is underappreciated and worthy of more praises and recognition than it has already received.<br /><br />Briefly, this is a picaresque adventure, following the tribulations of the busboy (waiter) Ditie in Bohemia, starting in the 1930's through the World War and into the communist upheaval in the aftermath of the war. First glimpsed as a crafty 14 y.o. selling hamburgers to time-pressed train travelers and conning them out of their money, we follow him through a series of jobs in luxurious hotels, culminating with a sumptuous soiree serving not the King of England, but Hailee Selassie - the Emperor of Ethiopia. <br /><br />A classic case of Napoleonic complex, Ditie's very short stature is constantly pushing him forward to prove himself and to demonstrate that he belongs, that he is as good, if not better than any of his colleagues and patrons. His ultimate ambition is to become a hotel owner himself, serving the rich and famous and basking in the recognition of his peers. Ditie's lack of scruples, amoral atitudes and self absorbtion might have turned me off towards him if it weren't for his exuberant zest for life, manifest in his early discovery and veneration of a woman's body (<i> breasts swinging like bells in a church tower </i>), in the carefree throwing away of money on rich food, drinks and wild parties. For me the book is a happy marriage between the rigorous Germanic pride in a job well done and impecable service with the wilder Slav streak that lives for the moment and swings often between extremes of joy and depression. The closest analogy to the irreverent and whimsical style of Hrabal narration is coming from the movies : think of an amalgam of Federico Fellini's wild carnivals, the early biting satire of Milos Forman, Emir Kusturica dancing to gypsy music on the edge of the abyss, the melancholy and the lust in Francois Truffaut or Giuseppe Tornatore mannish boys. The inclusion of Bohumil Hrabal among these 'sacred monsters' doesn't seem forced to me, as I consider his work every bit as accomplished and relevant of human dreams and desires.<br /><br />The title of the novel is both misleading and difficult to pin down. As I already mentioned, Ditie doesn't actually service the King of England, yet the phrase comes to define his life, the turning point of his career, the eye opener and later the acceptance of his failures together with his successes. The first to use the phrase is one of Ditie's mentors, Mr. Skrivánek - who considers waiting an art which, he says, involves a deep knowledge of human nature. The payoff from these lessons and observations will come only when Ditie learns about pain and loss and the imminence of death, watching young men departing for the war, leaving their loved one behind (<i> I learned to see the countryside, the flowers on the tables, the children at play, and to see that every hour is a sacrament </i>)<br /><br />Other mentors in Ditie's waiting career will urge him to to keep his ears open without hearing, keep his eyes open without seeing. He will be ready to recognize and profit from every opportunity, while maintaining a facade of discretion and detachment from his client's debauchery. From a travelling salesman he learns about the true value of money, not in themselves but in the liberty of choices and in the hedonist delights they buy. 'Easy come, easy go' is another of those Balkanic angles that are familiar territory for me. Losing everything can be a liberating step, or as we say around here, a kick in the behind is a step forward:<br /><br /><i> Tommorrow I would live for somewhere far away, far from people, though of course I knew there'd be people there too, and I'd always believed, like everyone else who works in artificial light, that one day I would get out of the city and into nature, that when I retired I would see what a forest really looked like, what the sun really looked like, the sun that has shoned into my face every day of my life, making me shield my eyes with a hat or a shadow. When I was a waiter I used to love it when at least once a day all those doormen and superintendents and stokers would come out of their buildings, turn their faces upward, and from the abyss of the Prague streets gaze at the strip of sky overhead, at the clouds, to see what time really was, according to nature and not by the clock. </i><br /><br />The politics don't play a very big role in the beginning of the novel, but as Ditie grows up he can no longer ignore the events that will directly affect his life : the invasion of his native land while the woman he falls in love with is a Nazi activist, the atrocities against Jews in Poland that will provide him with the means to buy his dream hotel; Lidice - the village razed to the ground in revenge for partizan activity, the proud Aryan supermen returning crippled and empty-eyed from the Russian front, the communist regime upsetting the social ladder that Ditie had tried so hard to climb. The humorous overtones and the epicurean exploits never fully disappear from the landscape (see a memorable late scene in an improvised prison), but the pain, the injustice and the futility of existence would mark the later journey of our picaresque hero. I have chosen to interpret Ditie's final reiteration of the boast from the title, as him looking back at his life not in anger but with a clear eye, no regrets or excuses or moral lessons, not even any grandiose revelation about the meaning of life, just a bit of justifiable pride and a half smile at the folly and beauty of it all.<br /><br /><i> The only right things were the things I enjoyed - not the way children and drunks enjoy things, but the way the professor of French literature taught me, enjoyment that was metaphysical. When you enjoy something, then you've got it, you idiots, you evil, stupid, criminal sons of men, he would say, and he'd browbeat us until he got us where he wanted us, open to poetry, to objects, to wonder, and able to see that beauty always points to infinity and eternity. </i><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11457099063340944885" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_173081736" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/173081736" />
    <a title="Lena" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/220791-lena"><img alt="Lena" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1589815535p2/220791.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/173081736?book_show_action=true">Jun 02, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lena" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lena" href="/user/show/220791-lena">Lena</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/220791-lena?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer173081736" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17477012642041582949">This is a highly regarded mid-century novel by an acclaimed Czech writer that wasn't translated into English until late last century. It tells the story of Ditie, an impoverished youth who starts work selling hot dogs in a train station and works his way up through the service industry as life around him is torn asunder by the second World War.<br/><br/>I wasn't quite sure what to make of this novel at first, with its page-long sentences, dearth of female characters and absurdist, fairy-tale undertones. B</span>
  <span id="freeText17477012642041582949" style="display:none">This is a highly regarded mid-century novel by an acclaimed Czech writer that wasn't translated into English until late last century. It tells the story of Ditie, an impoverished youth who starts work selling hot dogs in a train station and works his way up through the service industry as life around him is torn asunder by the second World War.<br /><br />I wasn't quite sure what to make of this novel at first, with its page-long sentences, dearth of female characters and absurdist, fairy-tale undertones. But as I got deeper into the book, the innocent voice of the determined Ditie began to grow on me. As he moved through the ranks of restaurant service, I also got caught up in the story of the German invasion as seen through the eyes of a Czech who had the luck/misfortune to fall in love with a German nurse.<br /><br />Though buffeted on the winds of fate both good and bad, Ditie comes through it all with a remarkably clear-eyed understanding of his place in history. I closed the book with a great deal of affection for this wise fool of a character and the insights he shared about his unusual life. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17477012642041582949" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1844572635" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1844572635" />
    <a title="Laysee" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4863523-laysee"><img alt="Laysee" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1459741824p2/4863523.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1844572635?book_show_action=true">Dec 20, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laysee" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laysee" href="/user/show/4863523-laysee">Laysee</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1844572635" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13778681999428636493">Czech writer, Bohumil Hrabal, is a raconteur par excellence. “I Served the King of England” is a highly entertaining story about how the unbelievable comes true many times over for Ditě. When we first make his acquaintance, he is only age 15 and a busboy at the Grand Prague Hotel. The story follows Ditě’s colorful career in the hotel industry from busboy to waiter, to lead waiter and to hotel owner; his sexual exploits; his self-awakening. In essence, it is the story of Ditě, a pint-size man try</span>
  <span id="freeText13778681999428636493" style="display:none">Czech writer, Bohumil Hrabal, is a raconteur par excellence. “I Served the King of England” is a highly entertaining story about how the unbelievable comes true many times over for Ditě. When we first make his acquaintance, he is only age 15 and a busboy at the Grand Prague Hotel. The story follows Ditě’s colorful career in the hotel industry from busboy to waiter, to lead waiter and to hotel owner; his sexual exploits; his self-awakening. In essence, it is the story of Ditě, a pint-size man trying to find his way in the world and to gain respect. The novel also offers glimpses of life in Czechslovakia before and after the German occupation in World War II. I derived much pleasure from Hrabal’s vivid depiction of the otherwise invisible operations behind buzzling hotels and the unimaginable cuisine (lung in sour sauce, anyone?) served in their fancy restaurants. The novel is uproariously funny (for a good stretch) and yet it is unsuspectingly profound in its observation of our human aspirations and needs.<br /><br />Hrabal’s character caricatures are memorable and a joy to read. I grew to love a few of the key waiters who are influential in mentoring Ditě and honing his skills of observation. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Chief among them is Mr. Skrivanek, the lead waiter in Hotel Paris, who claims to have served the King of England. There is the all-seeing wheelchair bound boss who commandeers his staff manically with a whistle. In the same Hotel Tichota is a giant porter who splits wood with "melodic blows of his ax" in the flood lit courtyard only when guests arrive. The salesmen who dined and traded at the Grand Prague Hotel are a hoot. There is the unforgettable red-caped general, a big-spending guest at Hotel Tichota, who vehemently derides every dish but consumes each with relish.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />An intoxicating quality permeates Hrabal’s prose and the settings that he paints. There is a sense of wild abandon ("free-spending gaiety and debauchery”), a celebration of wealth and the frivolity of literally throwing money to the four winds. The writing is cheeky, saucy, ribald. Then the narrative takes on a sober and darker tone as it segues into the German occupation of Czechoslovakia. Unpleasant events happen to Ditě but the unbelievable also keeps coming true for him.<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ The reader follows Ditě through his marriage, his phenomenal success in realizing his dream to be a hotelier, the humbling discovery that wealth does not guarantee social acceptance or respect, his internment in a “millionaire’s prison” after the communist takeover of Czechoslovakia, his enduring friendship with Zdenek (the former Hotel Tichota head waiter turned politician), his solitary life in the mountains with a dog, a horse, a goat, and a cat that is all the family he has. It is most gratifying that the Ditě we meet at the end stands tall and is able to laugh at himself.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />The novel is graced with many pleasing and touching episodes that merit re-reading. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The episode that stands out is the visit of the Emperor of Ethiopia to Hotel Paris and the feast of the stuffed camel that catapulted Ditě to distinction, an honor that served him for the rest of his life. There is an anecdote of a professor of French Literature teaching a frivolous young girl how to learn French and love books. The Goodreads community will love what I thought is a fitting tribute to the humanizing and transformative power of literature.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Read “I Served the King of England”. It exceeds expectations on many counts. Five brilliant stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13778681999428636493" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer275908182" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10339630557546675403">Great characters don't die: take Svejk for example. Last seen hunting mongrels to smuggle into the purebred boudoirs of gullible dowagers , he emerges, after a Van Winklian absence, in the novels and persona of Bohumil Hrabal, arguably central Europe's greatest pure novelist of the post WWII era (by "pure novelist" here I mean something like what sportswriters mean when they describe a basketball player as a "pure shooter" or "pure point guard": nobility, heredity, mystical powers). I Served the</span>
  <span id="freeText10339630557546675403" style="display:none">Great characters don't die: take Svejk for example. Last seen hunting mongrels to smuggle into the purebred boudoirs of gullible dowagers , he emerges, after a Van Winklian absence, in the novels and persona of Bohumil Hrabal, arguably central Europe's greatest pure novelist of the post WWII era (by "pure novelist" here I mean something like what sportswriters mean when they describe a basketball player as a "pure shooter" or "pure point guard": nobility, heredity, mystical powers). I Served the King of England is one of my favorite chapters in the Hrabalian human comedy: a deeply loveable masterpiece. It affirms without being coy and bubbles without being annoying, in part because B.H is never afraid of reminding us (as Hasek liked to) that his hero is an idiot. Not someone masquerading as an idiot, but an actual idiot. Does the trick sound familiar (Quixote, Mishkin, Homer Simpson)? Well then number Hrabal where he belongs, among Davenport's "secret Christians". Kind of like Friar Tuck. </span>

<div id="review_2126506586" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2126506586" />
    <a title="Jeannette Nikolova" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2574284-jeannette-nikolova"><img alt="Jeannette Nikolova" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480677087p2/2574284.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2126506586?book_show_action=true">Sep 17, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeannette Nikolova" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeannette Nikolova" href="/user/show/2574284-jeannette-nikolova">Jeannette Nikolova</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2126506586" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15463320992494892519"><a href='https://wondrousbooks.wordpress.com/2017/12/03/i-served-the-king-of-england-by-bohumil-hrabal/' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <i>Also available on the WondrousBooks blog.</i>
</a><br/><br/>I bought this book during my trip to Prague as part of my project to get a book or two in each new country I visit. Now, this is not my first Czech book, but I wanted to try a new Czech author nevertheless.<br/><br/>In my opinion, one of the best things about the book was actually the foreword. Unfortunately, I don't have the book right now, so I can't mention the author of the foreword, but they wrote a very informative, interesting and engaging analysis of bo</span>
  <span id="freeText15463320992494892519" style="display:none"><a href="https://wondrousbooks.wordpress.com/2017/12/03/i-served-the-king-of-england-by-bohumil-hrabal/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>Also available on the WondrousBooks blog.</i>
</a><br><br>I bought this book during my trip to Prague as part of my project to get a book or two in each new country I visit. Now, this is not my first Czech book, but I wanted to try a new Czech author nevertheless.<br><br>In my opinion, one of the best things about the book was actually the foreword. Unfortunately, I don't have the book right now, so I can't mention the author of the foreword, but they wrote a very informative, interesting and engaging analysis of both "<strong>I Served the King of England</strong>", and Hrabal's literature in general. Not knowing the author, it really helped me see some tendencies in his writing and in the themes he uses.<br><br>The book itself was not exactly to my liking. The story was rather interesting, but the atmosphere was very tight and suffocating. The main character was such a narrow-minded little man that his world was equally as small and claustrophobic. His experiences, even the ones he was most proud of and most happy about, always had a pinch of wrongness and just this general feeling about something dirty and repulsive happening. For example, as you can see in the cover of the book, he liked to put flowers in the pubic hair of the women he slept with. But those women were either prostitutes, or his Nazi-to-the-bone wife, and there was something very unpleasant and private about reading about his joy from this action.<br><br>I feel like this is something that often happens in European literature, and especially that of the ex-Communist countries. While in American literature even murder and gore are kind of shiny in description, in European literature, there is this sense of the author wanting to create shock in the reader through showing the reality in the most vulgar way possible. It is a thing I have always noticed in in every piece of art in Bulgaria - be it literature, movies, paintings, there is always sex. But it is not appealing, erotic sex. It's always the kind of description of sex which makes you feel uncomfortable and in need of a hot shower and a lot of scrubbing.<br><br>This is how I felt while reading this book. And spoiler alert for my next Czech book - <strong>The Joke</strong>, - same thing there.<br><br>One thing which was mentioned in the foreword of the book which I couldn't help but notice later on, was the fact that the character is always in need of proving himself and he is in a desperate need of attention and achieving every physical element of happiness and obtaining every material proof of success. While in character he is a spineless worm, in aspiration, he wants, and even briefly manages, to be rich and famous.<br><br>Setting everything about the story aside, Hrabal, undeniably, has a very good writing style. The descriptions he uses are very poetical and thought through. He guides the reader into his world and helps him see everything through exactly the right prism.<br><br><blockquote>
  <em>"I knew for certain that this girl could never be happy, but that her life would be sadly beautiful, and that life with her would be both an agony and a fulfillment for a man."</em>
  <br>
</blockquote><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1515004815ra/24824230.gif" alt="tenor" width="498" height="203" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="15463320992494892519" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_573248130" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/573248130" />
    <a title="Steven" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4452312-steven"><img alt="Steven" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1449093910p2/4452312.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/573248130?book_show_action=true">Mar 28, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven" href="/user/show/4452312-steven">Steven</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4452312-steven?shelf=books-i-own">books-i-own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4452312-steven?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4452312-steven?shelf=czech">czech</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer573248130" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6423217286087822666"><blockquote><i>"But I didn't want to be seen by human eyes anymore, or praised for what I'd done—all of that had left me."</i> (226)</blockquote>I lived in Prague for almost seven years, from the age of nine to fourteen. I was too young at the time to have a sense of Czech literature, of course, but I'm trying to make up for it – retrospectively – by reading more Czech authors (aside from the more obvious choice of Kundera). This actually goes for the literatures of all the countries in which I've lived; sadly, I only started </span>
  <span id="freeText6423217286087822666" style="display:none"><blockquote><i>"But I didn't want to be seen by human eyes anymore, or praised for what I'd done—all of that had left me."</i> (226)</blockquote>I lived in Prague for almost seven years, from the age of nine to fourteen. I was too young at the time to have a sense of Czech literature, of course, but I'm trying to make up for it – retrospectively – by reading more Czech authors (aside from the more obvious choice of Kundera). This actually goes for the literatures of all the countries in which I've lived; sadly, I only started seriously reading when I came back to the Netherlands to study. Anyway, this was my first encounter with Hrabal. All in all it was a memorable novel – I especially liked how he managed to condense significant elements of modern Czech history (Sokolism, pre-war anti-German sentiments, WWII itself, post-war communism) in the life of a rather ordinary character. The beginning and ending were strong and compelling, but there was a large part in the middle that left me unaffected, though. But I'll definitely read more by Hrabal – his style was rather unique. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6423217286087822666" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText9665387493929431486" style="display:none">Really strong stream-of-consciousness novel that plays fast and loose with time and stakes. The first half is especially strong - the action in various hotels is hilarious and well-observed, and Dite is that rare mixture of likeable and abhorrent. The political intersections (both World War II and the rise of Communism) are both interesting and obligatory, but I do think they occasionally slowed down the action and contributed to the general feeling of trailing off that pervaded. The ending, though a totally bizarre decision, is one I can get behind. As a whole, this thing is exceedingly devourable.     <a title="Mike Nettleton" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10949167-mike-nettleton"><img alt="Mike Nettleton" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer368250742" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7641056696620475091">I don't read much fiction these days, but when I do, I often go for or back to Hrabal. Even though Kundera is probably the best known Czech writer (and another favorite) Hrabal is considered the grandaddy. They cover many of the same themes — communism, relationships, sex — but their styles couldn't be more different. Hrabal reminds me of Hemingway via Bukowski — short, boozy and punchy. But he also mixes in a dream-like quality, where implausible things are made to seem commonplace. <br/><br/>This was my</span>
  <span id="freeText7641056696620475091" style="display:none">I don't read much fiction these days, but when I do, I often go for or back to Hrabal. Even though Kundera is probably the best known Czech writer (and another favorite) Hrabal is considered the grandaddy. They cover many of the same themes — communism, relationships, sex — but their styles couldn't be more different. Hrabal reminds me of Hemingway via Bukowski — short, boozy and punchy. But he also mixes in a dream-like quality, where implausible things are made to seem commonplace. <br /><br />This was my first read of Hrabal and probably still my favorite. I've read that much of what makes Hrabal so great is lost in the translation. I'll never know but the english translation of I Served the King of England nails that dreamy chance-happenings quality. This book will make you start thinking about booking a trip to Prague. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7641056696620475091" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2518386009" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2518386009" />
    <a title="Andrew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/442654-andrew"><img alt="Andrew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1513093267p2/442654.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2518386009?book_show_action=true">Sep 13, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrew" href="/user/show/442654-andrew">Andrew</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/442654-andrew?shelf=eastern-european-fiction">eastern-european-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/442654-andrew?shelf=czech-fiction">czech-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2518386009" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14438410443033433381">I know the (excellent) work of Stefan Zweig was the direct inspiration of The Grand Budapest Hotel, but it's almost difficult to believe that I Served the King of England didn't play some role. After all, it's the story of the remarkable misadventures of a Middle European hotelier who tries to cope with Nazism, Bolshevism, and his own piss-poor luck. The style is similar to the other Hrabal books I've read, tragicomic and weirdly sweet even at its darkest, very much in the mode of writers like Z</span>
  <span id="freeText14438410443033433381" style="display:none">I know the (excellent) work of Stefan Zweig was the direct inspiration of The Grand Budapest Hotel, but it's almost difficult to believe that I Served the King of England didn't play some role. After all, it's the story of the remarkable misadventures of a Middle European hotelier who tries to cope with Nazism, Bolshevism, and his own piss-poor luck. The style is similar to the other Hrabal books I've read, tragicomic and weirdly sweet even at its darkest, very much in the mode of writers like Zweig and Gregor von Rezzori who tried to capture the complexities and the faded aristocracy of life in the ashes of the Austro-Hungarian Empire, and who are renowned in their homelands, but less so in the English-speaking world. Which sucks, because the same sensibility that appreciates Wes Anderson will probably appreciate this.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14438410443033433381" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1078129814" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1078129814" />
    <a title="Andrea" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1079443-andrea"><img alt="Andrea" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442532375p2/1079443.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1078129814?book_show_action=true">Oct 12, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrea" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrea" href="/user/show/1079443-andrea">Andrea</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099540939" href="/book/show/6338522-i-served-the-king-of-england">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1079443-andrea?shelf=in-translation">in-translation</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1078129814" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13984514489038815755">This book I love, the trials and tribulations of a tiny waiter, Ditie. A keen and amoral observer of human foibles in a rapid flow of tale telling that is almost stream of consciousness. There are plenty of whores in this book, and they are always happy and smiling in this book. It works from Ditie's perspective though, as it is never anything less than deeply flawed. Foundationally, it is written by someone who understands what it means to be on your feet all day, which won me over from the beg</span>
  <span id="freeText13984514489038815755" style="display:none">This book I love, the trials and tribulations of a tiny waiter, Ditie. A keen and amoral observer of human foibles in a rapid flow of tale telling that is almost stream of consciousness. There are plenty of whores in this book, and they are always happy and smiling in this book. It works from Ditie's perspective though, as it is never anything less than deeply flawed. Foundationally, it is written by someone who understands what it means to be on your feet all day, which won me over from the beginning because it does feel like this: <br /><blockquote>That was the other side of my tuxedo, the other side of all waiters and busboys and maître d's the world over: white starched shirts and dazzling white rubber collars and legs slowly turning black, like some horrible disease where people start dying from the feet up (14).</blockquote><br />I hate violence, but could love people who fought like this over romanticism and realism<br /><blockquote>They'd have discussions and read books, and they even put on plays, but about every two weeks the boss's wife would into passionate arguments with her husband, and they would disagree so violently about romanticism or realism or Smetana and Janáček that they would start pouring wine over each other and hitting each other.</blockquote><br />And Zdeněk? Who goes on to join the resistance while Ditie falls in love with a German woman in love with the Third Reich and lives ashamed and safe? I love the thought of a man and world where such great hearted generosity exists, just as I love dancing until morning:<br /><blockquote>He had an aristocratic habit of spending practically everything he earned, and now and then he would treat himself the way our guests did, but he'd always have so much money left over that toward mrning, when we came back by taxi, he's arouse the innkeeper of the emtiest inn in the village and order him to go wake up some musicians to play for him. Then Zdeněk would go from door to door and invite the sleepers to come down to the inn and drink his health, and then the music played and there was dancing until dawn , and when they'd drained the innkeepers bottles and barrels dry, Zdeněk would wake up the owner of the grocery store, buy a whole basketful of jars, and pas them out as gifts to all the old women and men (71-72).</blockquote><br />There is the incredible tale of the visit of Ethiopian emperor Haile Selassie, his 300 guests eating with cutlery of gold, the minute descroptions of the feast prepared for them of turkeys stuffed into antelope stuffed into a camel, so delicious the king's official taster jumps to his feet and shouts for joy. And Ditie is given a medal and sash for his service to the emperor which he wears ever after. <br /><br />After Ditie returns to his home with the dirty money stolen by his wife, becomes a millionaire but never part of the club, loses it all under the communists, is exiled to a kind of work camp, there is poetry here too:<br /><blockquote>In the morning the dew was so thick it tore like a curtain when you walked through it, and it fell in a kind of rosary on every blade of grass and every leaf, and if you just brushed again a branch the dew dropped off like pearls from a broken necklace (211)</blockquote><br />The spruce that sings, and whose wood is used to make instruments. Ditie's awakening into an intellectual world (along with a sulky slut who is made beautiful through this transformation but never mind):<br /><blockquote>The only right things were the things I enjoyed -- not the way children or drinkers enjoyed things, but the way the professor of French literature taught me, enjoyment that was metaphysical. When you enjoy something, then you've got it you idiots, you evil, stupid, criminal sons of me, he would say, and he;d browbeat us until he got us where he wanted us, open to poetry, to objects, to wonder, and able to see that beauty always points to infinity and eternity.</blockquote><br /><br />I liked the introduction, but always hate it when it doesn't include some thoughtful consideration of the translation. There is only a page at the back for translator Paul Wilson's acknowledgments, and he notes 'Bohumil Hrabal's work, Czechs say, is untranslateable. This book is my response to that challenge'. It goes on to call Hrabal's use of Czech 'Hrabalovština', which makes me rather yearn to read it as it was written. Or to read the many other novels, none of which have been translated. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13984514489038815755" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_608763478" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/608763478" />
    <a title="Bjorn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19667916-bjorn"><img alt="Bjorn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1367928200p2/19667916.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/608763478?book_show_action=true">May 06, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bjorn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bjorn" href="/user/show/19667916-bjorn">Bjorn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330308762" href="/book/show/148634.I_Served_the_King_of_England">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19667916-bjorn?shelf=czech">czech</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19667916-bjorn?shelf=czechoslovakia">czechoslovakia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer608763478" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17489900044005551841">Suffice to say that while it's not quite the bulls-eye that Too Loud A Solitude is, it still kept me absolutely riveted. Like with the other Hrabals I've read, it's a microperspective of something much larger, telling the story of life in Czechoslovakia from the 1930s to the communist era from the horizon of a small and rather clueless restaurant worker; he starts as a bus boy and works his way up to manager before everything comes crashing down, and yes, the double meaning of "serve" is very de</span>
  <span id="freeText17489900044005551841" style="display:none">Suffice to say that while it's not quite the bulls-eye that Too Loud A Solitude is, it still kept me absolutely riveted. Like with the other Hrabals I've read, it's a microperspective of something much larger, telling the story of life in Czechoslovakia from the 1930s to the communist era from the horizon of a small and rather clueless restaurant worker; he starts as a bus boy and works his way up to manager before everything comes crashing down, and yes, the double meaning of "serve" is very deliberate.<br /><br />Hrabal's comedy is anything but refined, at least on the surface; the first half is almost slapstick as he gleefully sends up the pre-war society that is still trying to pretend the old days of the Empire are still alive and well while still marvelling at new technology, and our narrator is young and selfish and learns to serve others by paying close attention to what they want and who they are; one look and he can tell exactly what they need. Then the war comes, in the middle of a sentence as if it doesn't have anything to do with him at first, and the comedy becomes increasingly dark; being a selfish sort, he picks the wrong side, falls in love with a German girl, has a tailormade Aryan baby (who turns out to be an imbecile) and sooner or later can't help but turn that sharpened sense of character perception towards himself.<br /><br />I Served The King Of England ends up both as sharp social criticism (it was banned in Czechoslovakia) and as a journey of self-discovery to rival (at least my memory of) Hesse's Siddharta. Except a lot funnier.<br /><br /><i>And I said to myself that during the day I would look for the road to the village, but in the evening I would write, looking for the road back, and then walk back along it and shovel away the snow that had covered my past, and so try, by writing, to ask myself about myself. </i></span>
  <a data-text-id="17489900044005551841" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3558957720" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3558957720" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3558957720?book_show_action=true">Sep 20, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim" href="/user/show/1895570-jim">Jim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780151457458" href="/book/show/2264253.I_Served_the_King_of_England">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1895570-jim?shelf=eastern-europe">eastern-europe</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1895570-jim?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3558957720" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13486662375712840283">This strange little book is like a course in meditation, teaching one how to live through hard times, from the Nazi invasion and Communism through to the present day. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/50071.Bohumil_Hrabal' title='Bohumil Hrabal' rel='nofollow noopener'>Bohumil Hrabal</a>'s hero, named something like Ditie, begins life as a waiter at a fancy hotel. Throughout the first half of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/19271031.I_Served_the_King_of_England__New_Directions_Classic_' title='I Served the King of England (New Directions Classic) by Bohumil Hrabal' rel='nofollow noopener'>I Served the King of England</a>, he moves from one fancy Czech hotel to the next, aiming to become a millionaire by carefully saving his money.<br/><br/>Being something of a shrimp, he is looked down by the tall hotel owners</span>
  <span id="freeText13486662375712840283" style="display:none">This strange little book is like a course in meditation, teaching one how to live through hard times, from the Nazi invasion and Communism through to the present day. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/50071.Bohumil_Hrabal" title="Bohumil Hrabal" rel="nofollow noopener">Bohumil Hrabal</a>'s hero, named something like Ditie, begins life as a waiter at a fancy hotel. Throughout the first half of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/19271031.I_Served_the_King_of_England__New_Directions_Classic_" title="I Served the King of England (New Directions Classic) by Bohumil Hrabal" rel="nofollow noopener">I Served the King of England</a>, he moves from one fancy Czech hotel to the next, aiming to become a millionaire by carefully saving his money.<br /><br />Being something of a shrimp, he is looked down by the tall hotel owners he encounters. It is only when he falls in love with a Sudeten German his own height that things begin to change. By then, Germany invades Czechoslovakia, and the Czechs are badly treated. But as he is blond and he is the grandson of the German-sounding Johann Ditie, he is accepted as an Aryan by the Nazis. He marries the German Lise at a Nazi civil ceremony, and she gives birth to a son, who turns out to be a cretin who compulsively drives nails by the hundreds into boards.<br /><br />When Lise dies in an Allied bombing, Ditie becomes rich from rare postage stamps Lise has stolen from Poles in the occupation. He builds a fancy hotel by a quarry, but still is viewed as a shrimp by his former bosses. But then Communism comes. Because he is a millionaire from the sale of the postage stamps to collectors, he is forced into a Communist prison for millionaires, where the inhabitants wind up enjoying themselves. <br /><br />But then all good things must come to an end. Ditie is made to become a forest laborer when the prison is closed, and then a solo road mender. It is now that he comes into his own. He thinks back to two former forest laborers: a French professor and a young woman named Marcela. "I thought about how the professor of French literature had told Marcela that the only true man of the world was one who could become anonymous, who could shed himself."<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13486662375712840283" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1295421806" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1295421806" />
    <a title="Stacey Jones" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22844362-stacey-jones"><img alt="Stacey Jones" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1375726767p2/22844362.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1295421806?book_show_action=true">May 31, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stacey Jones" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stacey Jones" href="/user/show/22844362-stacey-jones">Stacey Jones</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099540939" href="/book/show/6338522-i-served-the-king-of-england">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1295421806" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4398793574679074802">I Served the King of England is like no other novel I have ever read. It careens through the career of the narrator, Ditie, through the middle of the 20th century in Prague and the hinterlands of the Czech Republic, through multiple political systems and the ups and downs that go with them for the people on whose backs they rest. And yet its absurdism is delightful while it simultaneously diminishes what it challenges.<br/><br/>The style may take some getting used to My husband and I read this book aloud,</span>
  <span id="freeText4398793574679074802" style="display:none">I Served the King of England is like no other novel I have ever read. It careens through the career of the narrator, Ditie, through the middle of the 20th century in Prague and the hinterlands of the Czech Republic, through multiple political systems and the ups and downs that go with them for the people on whose backs they rest. And yet its absurdism is delightful while it simultaneously diminishes what it challenges.<br /><br />The style may take some getting used to My husband and I read this book aloud, and, honestly, my goal was just to read one paragraph in a sitting--they are that long! This style gives the book a frantic and frenetic pace, but it still charms and engages. There isn't much dialog, and what is there is included in the ever-hurtling-forward action as Ditie learns his waiting trade at multiple hotels, including the one where the head waiter served the King of England. Ditie gets his own big moment, which provides him with his own refrain through the ups and downs of the novel. <br /><br />I found this book so quintessentially Czech, and I loved the way it evokes a truly Czech sensibility of the randomness of life that isn't tied to self-worth or virtue (unlike the American dream). There is a Czech joke that makes a punchline out of a farmer showing a visitor two identical cows. He cannot stop extolling the virtues of one of the cows; he goes on and on and on. The other cow, when asked, he can say absolutely nothing good about. He hates that cow. He wishes it had never been born. He would starve it to death if he could afford its loss. The visitor can't stand it, and finally says, "But, aren't they exactly alike?!" The farmer answers, "Yes." <br /><br />This is the sensibility that comes through in this novel by Hrabal, and makes this novel such a beautiful and dear artifact of Czech culture and perspective. Random things happen all the time, and the beneficiaries of good are no more worthy than the targets of the bad. Ditie's life tumbles up and down, mostly due to circumstances around him, powered some by his own ambition. <br /><br />I also wondered if Wes Anderson didn't read this for inspiration for Grand Budapest Hotel. I could picture it in those terms so easily! <br /><br />This is a wonderful, fantastical, clever commentary on geopolitics and how they play in the lives of the "common man," and it is worth a read. Let yourself fall in--don't try to control it. Enjoy it as it washes over you. You'll be glad you did.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4398793574679074802" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
