  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1513645643" />
    <a title="Tsung" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4992851-tsung"><img alt="Tsung" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1298808605p2/4992851.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1513645643?book_show_action=true">Jan 17, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tsung" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tsung" href="/user/show/4992851-tsung">Tsung</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4992851-tsung?shelf=favourite">favourite</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1513645643" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12499432336026764179">The unbelievable came true.<br/><br/>“I served the King of England” is good old-fashioned storytelling and it goes straight to my favourites shelf. Hrabal is a master raconteur although his style may not appeal to everybody. It is rambling with long sentences and yarns which join together without a logical connection. Yet his stories are quirky, hilarious and grab your attention.<br/><br/>“When I started to work at the Golden Prague Hotel, the boss took hold of my left ear, pulled me up and said, You’re a busboy he</span>
  <span id="freeText12499432336026764179" style="display:none">The unbelievable came true.<br /><br />“I served the King of England” is good old-fashioned storytelling and it goes straight to my favourites shelf. Hrabal is a master raconteur although his style may not appeal to everybody. It is rambling with long sentences and yarns which join together without a logical connection. Yet his stories are quirky, hilarious and grab your attention.<br /><br />“When I started to work at the Golden Prague Hotel, the boss took hold of my left ear, pulled me up and said, You’re a busboy here, so remember, you don’t see anything and you don’t hear anything. Repeat what I just said. So I said I wouldn’t see anything and I wouldn’t hear anything. Then the boss pulled my right ear and said, But remember too that you’ve got to see everything and hear everything. Repeat it after me. I was taken aback, but I promised I would see everything and hear everything. That’s how I began.”<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The book chronicles the life story of a diminutive, young czech boy. He is such a non-entity that he remains almost nameless throughout. He is sometimes referred to as Ditie (“child” in Czech), after the name on his grandfather’s gravestone. He is self-conscious and insecure. His one desire is to find recognition in society. So compelling is his need that even when he could have avoided imprisonment during the Communist regime for being a millionaire, he quite adamantly insists on being counted among them.<br /><br />It starts off with his basic training as a busboy in the Golden Prague Hotel, his profiteering selling frankfurters at the train station and his sexual awakening in the whorehouse Paradise. Despite the dishonesty and the raunchiness of his sexual encounters, there is a certain naiveté about his encounters.<br /><br />He moves on to the Hotel Tichota, where he gets promoted to waiter and meets Zdenek the headwaiter, who would go on to cross paths with him and watch over him through the years.<br /><br />Again he moves on to the grand Hotel Paris and is taken under the wing of headwaiter Mr Srivanek, who once served the King of England. His time there culminates in an ostentatious feast for the Emperor of Ethiopia. It is at this pinnacle that Ditie comes in to his own, when he gets to emulate his exemplar, Mr Srivanek, by serving the Emperor of Ethiopia. He overcomes his inferiority complex and develops confidence in himself.<br /><br />“I learned that feeling victorious makes you victorious, and that once you lose heart or let yourself be discouraged the feeling of defeat will stay with you for the rest of your life…”<br /><br />Then the Germans take over the country. Ditie naively welcomes the Germans, even falling in love and marrying Lise, a Nazi gym teacher. He blindly believes that he will be treated with respect by the Germans. He soon learns the painful truth. He feels guilty and conflicted aligning with the Germans while his fellow countrymen were being executed. Ironically, the New Child he produces with Lise from their National Socialist intercourse turns out to be a cretin. Even then, they are still a family. That is until tragedy strikes.<br /><br />After the war, Ditie realizes his dream of owning a hotel, using money made from the sale of valuable stamps confiscated from Jews. His happiness is short lived as the newly established Communist state takes it all away from him. As he spends the remainder of his days as a labourer, he starts to reflect upon his life as well as his death. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />A few features stand out in the story.<br /><br />The writing style of each successive chapter parallels the bildungsroman of Ditie. It starts off in a light-hearted, comical, and juvenile way. It builds up to the absurd, Baron Munchausen-like feast of the Emperor of Ethiopia. Then the tone of the story becomes more solemn and unsettling with the arrival of the Germans. It changes again in towards the end as it moves away from rambling narratives to more descriptive, reflective and introspective style.<br /><br />“not the meaning of what used to be or what happened a long time ago, but discovering what kind of road you’d opened up and had yet to open, and whether there was still time to attain the serenity that would secure you against the desire to escape from your own solitude, from the most important questions that you should ask yourself.”<br /><br />“…I see how the people here were all simply enjoying themselves the way I used to enjoy myself, putting off the questions they would have to ask themselves one day, if they were lucky enough to have the time to do that before they died.”<br /><br />“…how we deal with our own death is the beginning of what is beautiful, because the absurd things in our lives, which always end before we want them to anyway, fill us, when we contemplate death, with bitterness and therefore with beauty.”<br /><br />“…man’s body and spirit are indestructible and he is merely changed or metamorphized.”<br /><br />The story was not as political as one would expect. While there was an obvious indictment of the Nazis, there was none brought against the Communists. The imprisonment of the millionaires by the Communist regime was more like a getaway retreat and a farce. Even his assignment by the state to perform manual labour in the loneliest of places, was not presented as an injustice but as something normal.<br /><br />Ditie remained curiously insular despite all the events surrounding him, whether on a personal or on a national level.<br /><br />One small sentence reminded me of another small part of “Augustus” by John Williams, where Augustus the Emperor is reminded of his childhood.<br /><br />“And so the circle began to close and I started going back to my childhood and my youth, and I was a busboy again, and at the same time I stood face to face with myself, forced to look at my life.”<br /><br />I was particularly drawn to the recurring theme of apprenticeship and mentorship. Ditie had two important influences in Zdenek and Mr Skrivanek. Even the professor of French Literature at the end played a part in Ditie’s education.<br /><br />Overall, this book is intriguing. On the surface, it seems ribald and frivolous. But it is actually much deeper than that, “this story of how the unbelievable came true”.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12499432336026764179" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/256673361" />
    <a title="Eugene" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/76519-eugene"><img alt="Eugene" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442490147p2/76519.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/256673361?book_show_action=true">Jan 05, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eugene" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eugene" href="/user/show/76519-eugene">Eugene</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer256673361" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9692470357310424844">reminded me of walser -- maybe a more worldy walser. as if instead of retreating to the madhouse, hrabal was sentenced to the purgatory of the diplomatic corps -- forced propriety despite the absurd or horrific swirls of history around him. but, like walser, he recognizes the poetic gesture... poetic or romantic despite or because of the old world sexism and classism rampant (and rampant still) just before the second world war, the ripened-to-rot but still shiny weimar-type decadence... without </span>
  <span id="freeText9692470357310424844" style="display:none">reminded me of walser -- maybe a more worldy walser. as if instead of retreating to the madhouse, hrabal was sentenced to the purgatory of the diplomatic corps -- forced propriety despite the absurd or horrific swirls of history around him. but, like walser, he recognizes the poetic gesture... poetic or romantic despite or because of the old world sexism and classism rampant (and rampant still) just before the second world war, the ripened-to-rot but still shiny weimar-type decadence... without mentioning it to spoil it, the first chapter has one of the more romantic scenes i've read in many a year. <br /><br />the movement from charming and bawdy to dark satire and political farce to apocalyptic dream and finally into prayerful meditation -- all that transition done quietly, even feigning modesty, yet this quiet hiding a great ambition. the transitions' build-up and execution reflective of not only the change of an individual but of nation-states. <br /><br />here's a scene to wet yer whistle:<br /><br /><b> "I saw Zdenëk, the headwaiter at the Hotel Tichota, who enjoyed having a good time so much when he was off work that to get it he'd spend all the money he had with him, which was always several thousand. Then I saw his uncle, a military bandmaster now retired, who split wood on his little plot of land in the forest where he had a cottage overgrown with flowers and wild vines. This uncle had been a bandmaster at the time of the Austro-Hungarian Empire and still wore his uniform when he split wood, because he had written two polkas and several waltzes that still got played all the time, although no one remembered who the composer was and everyone thought he'd died a long time ago. Zdenëk and I, as we were riding along in a rented buggy on one of our days off, heard the sound of a military brass band playing one of his uncle's waltzes, and Zdenëk stood up and signaled the driver to stop, then went over to the band and had a little talk with the bandmaster. He offered to give him all the money he had, four thousand crowns, for the soldiers to buy themselves beer, if they would do what he asked. Buses were waiting, and the whole band was getting ready to climb aboard to go to a band tattoo, so we left the buggy there and got on the first bus with them. After an hour's drive we stopped in a forest, and soon a hundred and twenty uniformed musicians with their shiny instruments were advancing slowly down a road through the woods. Then they turned onto a footpath lined with thick bushes and pine trees that towered overhead, and Zdenëk signaled them to stop and slipped through some loose planks in a fence, disappeared into the bushes for a few moments, then came back and told them his plan. When he gave the sign, the soldiers climbed one by one through the hole in the fence into the bushes while Zdenëk, like a soldier at the front, directed them to take positions around the tiny house. They could hear the sound of an ax striking wood, and the entire band silently surrounded the chopping block and an old man in an ancient Austrian bandleader's uniform. When Zdenëk gave the signal, the bandmaster flung his golden ceremonial baton in the air, gave a loud command, and out of the bushes rose a glistening array of brass instruments and the band began to play a clamorous polka by Zdenëk's uncle. The old bandleader stood transfixed over the piece of wood he had just split, while the band moved forward a couple of steps, still up to their waists in pine and oak shrubs. Only the bandmaster stood in the greenery up to his knees, swinging his golden baton while the band played the polka and their instruments flashed in the sunlight. The old bandleader slowly looked around with a heavenly expression on his face, and when they finished the polka the band started right in on one of his concert waltzes, and the old bandleader sat down, put his ax across his knees, and began to cry. The bandmaster came up and touched his shoulder, the old man looked up, and the bandmaster handed him the golden baton. Now the old man got to his feet and, as he told us afterward, he thought he'd died and gone to heaven with a military band all around him, and he thought they must play military music in heaven and that God Himself was conducting the band and was now turning His own baton over to him. So the old man conducted his own pieces, and when he'd finished, Zdenëk stepped out of the bushes, shook hands with his uncle, and wished him good health. Half an hour later the band climbed back into their buses and as they were driving away they played Zdenëk a farewell ceremonial fanfare. Zdenëk stood there filled with emotion and bowed and thanked them, and finally the buses, and with them the fanfares, faded down the road through the woods, lashed by beech branches and shrubs" (159-61).</b><br /><br /></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13200272965045838656">I came to this book thinking I could perhaps recover the feeling of Grand Budapest Hotel. I was wrong. But I still enjoyed it. It's a comic Epic about the life of a Czech in the 20th century, with all the surrealism and the grotesque afforded not only by the writing itself, but by the very real incidents that could befall a man living in Central Europe during that period. Like being captured by the Gestapo. <br/><br/>The last chapter is particularly good. It's where the main character finally realises tha</span>
  <span id="freeText13200272965045838656" style="display:none">I came to this book thinking I could perhaps recover the feeling of Grand Budapest Hotel. I was wrong. But I still enjoyed it. It's a comic Epic about the life of a Czech in the 20th century, with all the surrealism and the grotesque afforded not only by the writing itself, but by the very real incidents that could befall a man living in Central Europe during that period. Like being captured by the Gestapo. <br /><br />The last chapter is particularly good. It's where the main character finally realises that he doesn't really need the respect or recognition of others because Hell is indeed other people and we are our own Heaven.<br /> <br />Finally, kudos to the translator: <br /><br />"As a matter of fact whenever I was in the pub I realized that the basic thing in life is questioning death, wanting to know how we'll act when our time comes, and that death, or rather this questioning of death, is a conversation that takes place between infinity and eternity, and how we deal with our own death is the beginning of what is beautiful, because the absurd things in our lives, which always end before we want them to anyway, fill us, when we contemplate death, with bitterness and therefore with beauty."</span>

<div id="review_931486223" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/931486223" />
    <a title="Stacia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3917029-stacia"><img alt="Stacia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1316011190p2/3917029.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/931486223?book_show_action=true">May 08, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stacia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stacia" href="/user/show/3917029-stacia">Stacia</a>

<div id="review_2729051526" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2729051526" />
    <a title="Paul Weiss" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/51664350-paul-weiss"><img alt="Paul Weiss" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1453113069p2/51664350.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2729051526?book_show_action=true">Feb 25, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul Weiss" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul Weiss" href="/user/show/51664350-paul-weiss">Paul Weiss</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51664350-paul-weiss?shelf=general-fiction">general-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51664350-paul-weiss?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51664350-paul-weiss?shelf=humour">humour</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2729051526" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer154701066348865328"><b>A <i>"playful, social satire"</i> I find quite distasteful!</b><br/><br/>On a recent vacation to Prague, I asked the proprietor of a local English language bookstore to recommend a novel that would be a good souvenir of my visit to Prague. She enthusiastically recommended <b>I Served the King of England</b> which she characterized as a comedic satire and added the guarantee that I would be sure to love it ... wrong!<br/><br/>The story takes place in Czechoslovakia from 1935 to 1950 during the final days of the Austro-Hungarian Empir</span>
  <span id="freeText154701066348865328" style="display:none"><b>A <i>"playful, social satire"</i> I find quite distasteful!</b><br /><br />On a recent vacation to Prague, I asked the proprietor of a local English language bookstore to recommend a novel that would be a good souvenir of my visit to Prague. She enthusiastically recommended <b>I Served the King of England</b> which she characterized as a comedic satire and added the guarantee that I would be sure to love it ... wrong!<br /><br />The story takes place in Czechoslovakia from 1935 to 1950 during the final days of the Austro-Hungarian Empire when Czechoslovakia tragically loses its freedom first to the armies of Nazi Germany and then to the Communist regime under Marx and Lenin.<br /><br />Bohumil Hrabal's <b>I Served the King of England</b> is the story of a Ditie - a small man in every respect including stature - personal vision, abilities, scope of imagination, interests and even charitable inclinations to his fellow Czechs. It is the story of his rise from the bottom of the hospitality pecking order as a much maligned hotel busboy to a millionaire and the owner of his own unique hand-crafted hotel. It is the story of his fall from that pinnacle of wealth back into poverty as war takes away everything that he worked for including his wife, his son, his hotel, his wealth and the respect of his peers that he had unsuccessfully searched for all of his life.<br /><br />My personal impression of Ditie as a character is that he is small-minded and selfish, focused on little else than the accumulation of wealth by any means, his social position in the eyes of his peers and the satisfaction of a powerful libido. He began his rise to power by stealing change from customers and he completed the accumulation of his first million by selling rare stamps purloined from Jewish prisoners who had been transported to concentration camps. In short, I found Ditie to be a most unlikable protagonist.<br /><br />As a novel, <b>I Served the King of England</b> was irreverent, treating the horrific events of WW II and the Nazi and Communist occupation of Czechoslovakia with an outrageous, mocking comedic lightheartedness that was out of place and verging on the abhorrent.<br /><br />The only redeeming feature of the entire novel is the heartwarming and heartbreaking story of his love for Lise, a German girl, who is despised by all of Ditie's Czech acquaintances.<br /><br />Insofar as its being a souvenir of my visit to Prague goes ... suffice it to say that many Czechoslovakian place names were mentioned, but nothing about the narrative, the history, the description or the action in the novel was particularly evocative of the setting of such a magnificent city. But for the context of the war itself, the story could have happened anywhere at all!<br /><br />Overall, my impression is that <b>I Served the King of England</b> is a self-proclaimed literary novel that attempts to turn Ditie's flaws into something charming. I'm not buying it at all.<br /><br />Definitely not recommended.<br /><br />Paul Weiss</span>
  <a data-text-id="154701066348865328" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_27640956" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/27640956" />
    <a title="Dennis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/962322-dennis"><img alt="Dennis" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1430584962p2/962322.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/27640956?book_show_action=true">Jul 18, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dennis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dennis" href="/user/show/962322-dennis">Dennis</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330308762" href="/book/show/148634.I_Served_the_King_of_England">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer27640956&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer27640956" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9166943154027270356">I lived in the Czech Republic for a long time and a lot of the thinking behind this book was very familiar to me. To begin with, Hrabal was one of the few writers to not leave during communism as he wasn't political and also allowed his novels to be edited for communist purposes. (Not much choice, I suppose.) So this book really reflected the Czech "cleverness" which is to go along with whomever is in power. So, the Nazis were villains but the communists are trearted myuch more kindly when they </span>
  <span id="freeText9166943154027270356" style="display:none">I lived in the Czech Republic for a long time and a lot of the thinking behind this book was very familiar to me. To begin with, Hrabal was one of the few writers to not leave during communism as he wasn't political and also allowed his novels to be edited for communist purposes. (Not much choice, I suppose.) So this book really reflected the Czech "cleverness" which is to go along with whomever is in power. So, the Nazis were villains but the communists are trearted myuch more kindly when they appropriated power and property. The capitalism of the protagonist becomes an error in his thinking and he learns to appreciate what's really important, the land and the people. The book was really very funny and entertaining in the beginning but began to seem more of a rhapsodic ass-kissing by the end as he "learns" that all he dreamed of and accomplished really didn't matter because it involved love of wealth instead of love of the land. Political correctness, socialist style. Whatever the people do (such as kill his dog) is forgiven because they're the `people and they do it out of love. I began to gag on this in the last part of the book so maybe it's me but I think the book is worth reading for its style, at the very least, even if you don't buy into all it says.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9166943154027270356" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ensiform" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ensiform" href="/user/show/3843117-ensiform">Ensiform</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780679727866" href="/book/show/87281.I_Served_the_King_of_England">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3843117-ensiform?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer108822947" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3852334892938441076">Translated by Paul Wilson (1980). A rich stream of consciousness novel, about Dite, a waiter who moves up in the world of Czech hotels, until the Germans invade and he marries a German woman. It jarred me: at first an absurdist romp, admiration for living life to its fullest à la Kazantzakis, then extremely abruptly, political nightmares intrude, and tragedy (albeit blithely reported, blithely experienced) and bittersweet philosophical observations rear their heads. I laughed out loud at some of</span>
  <span id="freeText3852334892938441076" style="display:none">Translated by Paul Wilson (1980). A rich stream of consciousness novel, about Dite, a waiter who moves up in the world of Czech hotels, until the Germans invade and he marries a German woman. It jarred me: at first an absurdist romp, admiration for living life to its fullest à la Kazantzakis, then extremely abruptly, political nightmares intrude, and tragedy (albeit blithely reported, blithely experienced) and bittersweet philosophical observations rear their heads. I laughed out loud at some of Dite’s crazy, bawdy adventures and recollections, but the book ends on a thoughtful, somewhat sad, serene note. A rich and rewarding tale, extolling experience over the power of history.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3852334892938441076" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Gregory" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10114138-gregory"><img alt="Gregory" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1583197532p2/10114138.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2211631098?book_show_action=true">Dec 11, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gregory" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gregory" href="/user/show/10114138-gregory">Gregory</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2211631098" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5871796956091851439">It felt like -22 today and I'm off work with a cold I'm almost certain I will die from, but this morning I dragged my broken sinuses and the body holding it to Starbucks to read a chapter or two from this book my little brother recommended. I stayed for a caffiene buzzed three hours and read the majority of it all in one sitting. It's a page-turner and it's simple and FUN to read. <br/><br/>****<br/><br/>It mainly details a young short busboy (and later not-young, not a busboy, but still short) longing for fame and</span>
  <span id="freeText5871796956091851439" style="display:none">It felt like -22 today and I'm off work with a cold I'm almost certain I will die from, but this morning I dragged my broken sinuses and the body holding it to Starbucks to read a chapter or two from this book my little brother recommended. I stayed for a caffiene buzzed three hours and read the majority of it all in one sitting. It's a page-turner and it's simple and FUN to read. <br /><br />****<br /><br />It mainly details a young short busboy (and later not-young, not a busboy, but still short) longing for fame and fortune in the hotelier business. It reminds me a lot of Mordecai Richler's Duddy Kravitz, a boy with ambition, a boy with wide eyes for money and women, and although Hrabal doesn't state it, like Duddy, our narrator in this book is also a Schlemiel. The book, while funny as Mordecai Richler gets in his books, Hrabal also gets an ending that stands with any heartbreaking tale of longing for riches that F. Scott Fitzgerald ever touched too. This book feels so American in content, and that goes without even mentioning John Steinbeck as a character in the novel, although it is clearly a product of Hrabal's surroundings and the political state of his country.<br /><br />This book is simple and beautiful at surface level, but there's so much here if you want to dig and I always love that quality in books. <br /><br />****<br /><br />I like how Hrabal wrote wine as well. I'm a sucker for... writers writing about things I'd like them to write about.<br /><br /><i>I remember he loved to drink heavy wines-ports, Algerian wines, Malaga- and he drank them all with great seriousness and so slowly he hardly seemed to be drinking, and each sip seemed to make him more handsome, and he'd roll the wine around in his mouth for a while and gulp it as though he were trying to get down a chunk of apple. After each mouthful, he would quickly declare that the Sahara sun was in that wine.</i> p. 51<br /><br />and again later<br /><br /><i>For the first time in my life I saw an entire wall bristling with bottles of Heinkel Trocken and sparkling champagnes, from Veuve Cliquot to Weinhardt's of Koblenz, walls of Martell and Hennessey cognac, hundreds of bottles of Scotch whiskies of all kinds. I also saw rare Mosel and Rhine wines, and out own Bzenecka wine from Moravia, and Czech wines from Melnik and Zernoseky. As he walked from cellar to cellar Mr. Skrivanek would caress the bottle necks fondly, like an alcoholic, though as a matter of fact he didn't drink, at least I'd never seen him drink, and I suddenly realized I'd never seen him sit down either<i></i><br /><br /></i></span>
  <a data-text-id="5871796956091851439" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('XZblOfsg9AAobPpmnfMALtByr4Iwi3wXYk8Jv6T9ne80UwxW4Z2xYq3iDSeCahVGJeRb/p3EHpT+n0BeRpnbgg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('bXfmOiKr4sFx+S9a6tXwjr7j9opO+DdKG5nJspkkzkAEsg9VOBano/R32Bv1TOXmS3UC9uO3VcmHSYBTe0CILQ==')})
  }

<script>
//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_15821936'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15821936-jozova-hanule?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Jozova Hanule<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/2824455.Kv_ta_Leg_tov_?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Květa Legátová<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.35 avg rating &mdash; 654 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2002\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer5674484754272674778\">Příběh Jozovy Hanule uzavírá želarský cyklus spisovatelky Květy Legátové, která v roce 2001 vpadla na českou literární scénu jako meteor. Postavami této novely, situované do let druhé světové války, jsou opět vesničtí obyvatelé, které čtenáři znají z<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText5674484754272674778\" style=\"display:none\">Příběh Jozovy Hanule uzavírá želarský cyklus spisovatelky Květy Legátové, která v roce 2001 vpadla na českou literární scénu jako meteor. Postavami této novely, situované do let druhé světové války, jsou opět vesničtí obyvatelé, které čtenáři znají z předchozí povídkové sbírky Želary. V centru vyprávění je však tentokrát osud mladé lékařky, ukrývající se v zapadlé horské vesnici před nacistickým pronásledováním, jež zde v prostředí zcela odlišném od intelektuálního klimatu velkoměsta nachází hluboký milostný vztah k prostému vesnickému muži. Na motivy novely Jozova Hanule natočil režisér Ondřej Trojan podle scénáře Petra Jarchovského film Želary (2003) a kniha již byla přeložena do deseti jazyků. Květa Legátová, vl. jm. Věra Hofmanová (1919), vystudovala na brněnské univerzitě češtinu, němčinu, fyziku a matematiku. Dlouhá léta působila jako učitelka na různých valašských a slováckých školách, přičemž jako politicky nespolehlivá byla často překládána z jedné školy na jinou. Ze slováckých Kopanic také čerpá náměty příběhů povídkové sbírky Želary (Paseka 2001) a novely Jozova Hanule (Paseka 2002). Pod pseudonymem Věra Podhorná napsala též řadu rozhlasových her. Nejméně čtrnáct z těchto „dialogů“ bylo realizováno, dva jako televizní adaptace, knižně vyšly ve svazcích Pro každého nebe (2003), Posedlá a jiné hry (Paseka 2004) a Mimo tento čas (2008). Další drobnější prózy shrnují knihy Nic není tak prosté (2006) a Mušle a jiné odposlechy (2007). May, h<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText1773440180781690332\" style=\"display:none\">Often compared to Byron, Keats, Shelley, and Poe, called Lautreamont\'s &quot;elder brother&quot; by the Czech Surrealists, Karel Hynek Mácha (1810-1836) was the greatest Czech Romantic poet, and arguably the most influential of any poet in the language. May, his epic masterpiece, was published in April 1836, just seven months before his death. Considered the &quot;pearl&quot; of Czech poetry, it is a tale of seduction, revenge, and patricide. A paean as well to nature, the beauty of its music and its innovative use of language, expertly captured in this new translation by Marcela Sulak, has ensured the poem\'s lasting popularity. Scorned at first by the national revivalists of the 19th century for being &quot;un-Czech,&quot; Mácha was held up as a &quot;national&quot; poet by later generations, a fate which the interwar Czech avant-garde, who considered him a precursor, took it upon themselves to reverse.\n\nUnlike the other seminal 19th-century European poets, Mácha\'s work has been largely ignored in English translation. The present volume, the only available in English, provides the original Czech text in parallel and includes a series of illustrations by Jindřich Štyrský specifically created for the poem.<\/span>\n       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/50071.Bohumil_Hrabal">About Bohumil Hrabal</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/50071.Bohumil_Hrabal"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1255518886p3/50071.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/50071.Bohumil_Hrabal">Bohumil Hrabal
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
926
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer161566554350777811">Born in Brno-Židenice, Moravia, he lived briefly in Polná, but was raised in the Nymburk brewery as the manager's stepson. <br/><br/>Hrabal received a Law degree from Prague's Charles University, and lived in the city from the late 1940s on.<br/><br/>He worked as a manual laborer alongside Vladimír Boudník in the Kladno ironworks in the 1950s, an experience which inspired the "hyper-realist" texts he was writing at t</span>
  <span id="freeText161566554350777811" style="display:none">Born in Brno-Židenice, Moravia, he lived briefly in Polná, but was raised in the Nymburk brewery as the manager's stepson. <br /><br />Hrabal received a Law degree from Prague's Charles University, and lived in the city from the late 1940s on.<br /><br />He worked as a manual laborer alongside Vladimír Boudník in the Kladno ironworks in the 1950s, an experience which inspired the "hyper-realist" texts he was writing at the time. <br /><br />His best known novels were Closely Watched Trains (1965) and I Served the King of England. In 1965 he bought a cottage in Kersko, which he used to visit till the end of his life, and where he kept cats ("kočenky"). <br /><br />He was a great storyteller; his popular pub was At the Golden Tiger (U zlatého tygra) on Husova Street in Prague, where he met the Czech President Václav Havel, the American President Bill Clinton and the then-US ambassador to the UN Madeleine Albright on January 11th, 1994.<br /><br />Several of his works were not published in Czechoslovakia due to the objections of the authorities, including The Little Town Where Time Stood Still (Městečko, kde se zastavil čas) and I Served the King of England (Obsluhoval jsem anglického krále).<br /><br />He died when he fell from a fifth floor hospital where he was apparently trying to feed pigeons. It was noted that Hrabal lived on the fifth floor of his apartment building and that suicides by leaping from a fifth-floor window were mentioned in several of his books.<br /><br />He was buried in a family grave in the cemetery in Hradištko. In the same grave his mother "Maryška", step father "Francin", uncle "Pepin", wife "Pipsi" and brother "Slávek" were buried.<br /><br />He wrote with an expressive, highly visual style, often using long sentences; in fact his work Dancing Lessons for the Advanced in Age (1964) (Taneční hodiny pro starší a pokročilé) is made up of just one sentence. Many of Hrabal's characters are portrayed as "wise fools" - simpletons with occasional or inadvertent profound thoughts - who are also given to coarse humour, lewdness, and a determination to survive and enjoy oneself despite harsh circumstances. Political quandaries and their concomitant moral ambiguities are also a recurrent theme.<br /><br />Along with Jaroslav Hašek, Karel Čapek, and Milan Kundera - who were also imaginative and amusing satirists - he is considered one of the greatest Czech writers of the 20th century. His works have been translated into 27 languages.</span>
  <a data-text-id="161566554350777811" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

This partial loads on almost every page view.  The associated React component makes
a call to SignInPromptController#get to determine if the user should see the sign in interstial.
This is determined by how many signed out pagehits the user has executed an how recently they have
last seen the insterstitial.  If the controller responds indicating the popup should appear, the
